                .addSize([0, 0], [300, 50])

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201703472')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="c2a4caa4cec44424abd49dc26ed8c3b8"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c2a4caa4cec44424abd49dc26ed8c3b8">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e3c90fcf-9823-4ec0-8d32-b6f9a02677d7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '493CCFB489165175A87F9CB1140E81DB';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d50516588" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The red P anode for sodium ion batteries has attracted great attention recently due to the high theoretical capacity, but the poor intrinsic electronic conductivity and large volume expansion restrain its widespread applications. Herein, the red P is successfully encapsulated into the cube shaped sandwich-like interconnected porous carbon building (denoted as P@C-GO/MOF-5) via the vaporization–condensation method. Superior cycling stability (high capacity retention of about 93% at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles) and excellent rate performance (502 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>) can be obtained for the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode. The superior electrochemical performance can be ascribed to the successful incorporation of red P into the unique carbon matrix with large surface area and pore volume, interconnected porous structure, excellent electronic conductivity and superior structural stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201703472-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703472-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been considered as the state-of-the-art technology in high-energy batteries that has been widely used as power source for portable electronic devices.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0001_R_d50516580e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">1</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0002_R_d50516580e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a></span> However, for large scale application, LIBs still have many limitations due to the limited mineral reserves and high cost of lithium-based compounds.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0003_R_d50516580e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0004_R_d50516580e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></span> Recently, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) have been attracted much attention since sodium resource is natural abundant, environmental friendly and showing similar chemical properties with lithium ion batteries.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0005_R_d50516580e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></span> Many efforts have been made to explore high performance anode materials for sodium ion batteries. Among various anode materials for NIBs, red phosphorus (P) is an attractive material as anodes due to its super high theoretical capacity (2596 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0006_R_d50516580e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem.">6</a>-<a href="#smll201703472-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0008_R_d50516580e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett.">8</a></span> However, the main challenges of the P anodes associated with structural degradation caused by the large volume change and low electronic conductivity (1 × 10<sup>−14</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0009_R_d50516580e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Electrochem. Commun.">9</a></span> The intrinsic low electronic conductivity of red P leads to the poor electronic kinetics, large polarization and further low active material utilization. In addition, the large volume expansion (≈400%) during Na insertion/extraction in red P leads to pulverization of red P and loose contact between the red P and the current collector, resulting in the severe capacity fading.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0010_R_d50516580e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ChemElectroChem">10</a>-<a href="#smll201703472-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0012_R_d50516580e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></span></p>
         
         <p>In order to improve the long cycling performance of red P electrode, great efforts have been mainly focused on the following two aspects: mitigating the pulverization of red P and improving its electronic conductivity. First, reducing the bulk red P particles to nanoscale is confirmed to be an effectively method to minimize the large volume change without cracking.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0013_R_d50516580e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#smll201703472-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0015_R_d50516580e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></span> Kim et al.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0013_R_d50516580e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></span> reported the amorphous red P and carbon black composite on a nanoscale could be successfully obtained through a ball milling process. The nanosized structure and the improved electronic conductivity enabled superior cycling stability without obvious capacity fading over 30 cycles. In the results of Jiao and co-workers,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0014_R_d50516580e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></span> nanosized red P particles could be obtained through ball milling and further a feasible shear emulsifying method, demonstrating superior sodium storage performance. Second, the encapsulation of red P nanoparticles into the porous carbon matrix to realize the superior long cycling stability is confirmed to be an attractive method in recent studies.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0014_R_d50516580e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a>-<a href="#smll201703472-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0018_R_d50516580e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a></span> Li et al.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0016_R_d50516580e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a></span> embedded the nanosized amorphous red P particles in the highly ordered mesoporous carbon (CMK-3) through the vaporization–condensation method, leading to the amorphous red P nanoparticles distributed in the porous carbon matrix homogeneously. The channels and the interconnected porous structure are beneficial for the diffusion of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> and alleviation the large volume expansion of red P nanoparticles simultaneously, which induced better cycling performance. Among these carbonaceous, graphene is considered as the most promising carbon matrix due to the high electrical conductivity, high chemical stability, and good flexibility.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0019_R_d50516580e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Lett.">19</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0020_R_d50516580e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></span> Pei et al.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0021_R_d50516580e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ChemElectroChem">21</a></span> reported the graphene scrolls encapsulated with a high content of 52.2% of phosphorus particles, showing a high reversible specific capacity and stable cycling performance over 150 cycles. The satisfying electrochemical performance is attributed to the unique conductive matrix of graphene. In Jiao and co-workers,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0014_R_d50516580e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></span> the ultrasmall grains of red P nanoparticles were successfully embedded into the highly porous N-doped carbon nanofibers through electrospinning and directly used as the free-standing anode for NIBs. The interconnected 3D porous network structure, high electronic conductivity and homogeneous encapsulation of red P nanoparticles into the unique carbon matrix provided a guarantee for the excellent sodium storage performance with fast electron and sodium ions transportation and the alleviation of pulverization of red P active material. The above results demonstrated that the synergistic effects of the high specific surface area, interconnected porous structure, good structural stability and excellent electronic conductivity of the carbon matrix could effectively provide the red P active material with adequate space to accommodate the volume expansion and much improved electronic conductivity to ensure fast electron/sodium ion diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, when used as anode material for energy storage, porous carbon derived from metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) displays many advantages, such as tunable porous structure, high specific surface area and multi-intersecting open channel system.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0022_R_d50516580e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">22</a>-<a href="#smll201703472-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0026_R_d50516580e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a></span> Among various kinds of MOFs family, MOF-5 is a kind of Zn-based MOF with a cube shaped porous structure.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0027_R_d50516580e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0028_R_d50516580e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Carbon">28</a></span> So the porous carbon derived from the carbonization of MOF-5 could maintain the cube shape and display unique porous structure with micropores and mesopores, ultrahigh surface area and high electronic conductivity, which is regarded as a promising carbon matrix for loading red P.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0029_R_d50516580e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">29</a></span></p>
         
         <p>In order to combine the synergistic effects of graphene and porous carbon matrix, we rational designed the unique carbon matrix derived from the carbonization of the stacking of graphene and MOF-5 layer by layer (denoted as C-GO/MOF-5) to load red P through the vaporization–condensation method.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0030_R_d50516580e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Energy">30</a></span> The unique structure shows a variety of advantages for the improved electrochemical performance for red P electrode: (i) The combination of the graphene sheets and the porous carbon could largely improve the electronic conductivity, facilitating the electron and sodium ion diffusion and enhancing the utilization of red P active material. (ii) The interconnected porous structure, high surface area and large pore volume could encapsulate red P within its pores and improve the integrity of red P during the repeated sodiation/desodiation process. (iii) The graphene sheets throughout the cube shaped network structure are beneficial for the structure integrity and stability. As a result, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode demonstrates superior sodium storage performance including excellent cycling stability (high capacity retention of about 93% at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles) and outstanding rate performance (502 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201703472-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703472-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>In the experimental process, first, the C-GO/MOF-5 porous carbon matrix could be obtained according to the results reported by Fan and co-workers. Then, as illustrated in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0007">1</a>, in order to load red P in the pores of the C-GO/MOF-5, the red P powders and C-GO/MOF-5 were mixed together and further operated by the vaporization–condensation method to lead to the formation of P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0031_R_d50516580e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0032_R_d50516580e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></span> The mixed composite was sealed in a quartz tube under vacuum and further heat treated at 898 °C for 8 h for ensuring effective diffusion of P vapor into the pores of carbon. Then the temperature was maintained at 260 °C for 24 h with a rate of 1 °C min<sup>−1</sup> to realize the fully transformation of white P to red P.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703472-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13a3ac0d-2bd2-45ff-bf17-7d849e190516/smll201703472-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/13a3ac0d-2bd2-45ff-bf17-7d849e190516/smll201703472-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1c3480a7-2b77-4159-9b94-7bb0750b2014/smll201703472-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13a3ac0d-2bd2-45ff-bf17-7d849e190516/smll201703472-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703472-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703472" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the encapsulation of red P into the interconnected porous carbon building.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of C-GO/MOF-5 are shown in Figure S1a,b (Supporting Information). The C-GO/MOF-5 displays a cube shaped structure consisting of a stacking of graphene and porous carbon derived from MOF-5 layer by layer. The graphene sheets throughout the cube shaped structure not only serves as the conductive network but also the support of the porous carbon network, which is beneficial for the improved electronic conductivity and further facilitating the sodium ion diffusion.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0017_R_d50516580e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0021_R_d50516580e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ChemElectroChem">21</a></span> Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images in Figure S1c,d of the Supporting Information also clearly exhibit cube shaped porous carbon network structure combining the porous carbon and the graphene sheets, which further confirms the layer by layer structure.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0030_R_d50516580e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Energy">30</a></span> The graphene sheets in the porous network structure are beneficial for the structure integrity and stability,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0030_R_d50516580e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Energy">30</a></span> providing structural stability for electrochemical reaction. After loading of red P, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0001">1</a>a–c,d–f) remains the similar morphology like the C-GO/MOF-5, demonstrating that red P is well encapsulated into 3D interconnected porous network.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0016_R_d50516580e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0018_R_d50516580e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a></span> Besides, from the edges and the sides of the cube shaped carbon shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0001">1</a>c, the thin platelets assembling from the graphene layer and the carbon layer could be observed, with the thickness of about 30 nm. The scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) images of P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite and the corresponding elemental mapping of C and P elements in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0001">1</a>g–i display the homogeneous distribution of P element in the cube shaped porous carbon matrix, further indicating that the red P is uniformly encapsulated into the porous carbon matrix.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0018_R_d50516580e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a></span> The change of the specific surface area and pore-size distribution of the C-GO/MOF-5 porous carbon before and after the encapsulation of red P are further measured through the nitrogen Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) adsorption measurements. As shown in Figure S2a,b (Supporting Information), the C-GO/MOF-5 porous carbon displays high BET surface area almost up to 1313.05 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> with abundant micropores and mesopores distributed at 1.11 and 3.27 nm, respectively. After the encapsulation of red P into porous carbon, the BET surface area dramatically decreases to 23.21 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and the total pore volume also decreases from 1.264 to 0.155 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> accompanying with the disappear of pores, indicating that the red P successfully occupies the pores in the C-GO/MOF-5 carbon matrix.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0016_R_d50516580e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0018_R_d50516580e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0033_R_d50516580e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mater. Chem. A">33</a></span> The red P content in the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite is 44.5% calculated by thermogravimetric analysis under nitrogen atmosphere (Figure S3, Supporting Information), almost two times higher than the content in the previous work of Li et al.,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0016_R_d50516580e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a></span> indicating this unique sandwiched porous carbon structure is beneficial for maintaining the loading level of P during the vaporization–condensation process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703472-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d8c96dd3-6ef0-4c04-affb-684e7d323259/smll201703472-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d8c96dd3-6ef0-4c04-affb-684e7d323259/smll201703472-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/848d1ada-3aa6-4f55-bf56-3fc89f06a29c/smll201703472-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d8c96dd3-6ef0-4c04-affb-684e7d323259/smll201703472-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703472-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703472" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) SEM images of the P@C-GO/MOF-5; d–f) TEM images of the P@C-GO/MOF-5; scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) images of g) P@C-GO/MOF-5 and corresponding elemental mapping of h) C and i) P elements.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the red P, the C-GO/MOF-5, and the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0002">2</a>a. For the red P, two major peaks located at 15° and 34.5° are the characteristic peaks of monoclinic phosphorus, belonging to the (013) and (31−8) planes,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0016_R_d50516580e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0034_R_d50516580e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a></span> corresponding to the typical peaks in Raman spectra ranging from 300 to 500 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0002">2</a>b).<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0034_R_d50516580e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a></span> While the C-GO/MOF-5 sample displays an amorphous structure. When incorporated with red P, both the XRD pattern and the Raman spectra of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite (Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0002">2</a>a,b) demonstrates the characteristics of the combination of red P and the C-GO/MOF-5. Moreover, the weaker peaks of red P for the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite indicate that the red P is confined into the pores of the carbon matrix.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0032_R_d50516580e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0034_R_d50516580e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703472-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/294d25a8-682d-44b5-9518-f8edb7a342b7/smll201703472-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/294d25a8-682d-44b5-9518-f8edb7a342b7/smll201703472-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d1a0853d-8a5d-4eef-9fad-d19994d0ed89/smll201703472-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/294d25a8-682d-44b5-9518-f8edb7a342b7/smll201703472-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703472-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703472" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of the red P, C-GO/MOF-5, and P@C-GO/MOF-5; b) Raman spectroscopy of the red P, C-GO/MOF-5, and P@C-GO/MOF-5.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The sodium storage performances of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode were evaluated in sodium half-cells. The electrochemical behavior of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode was investigated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) with a scan rate of 0.2 mV S<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 2 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0003">3</a>a, the broad irreversible peaks located at around 0.75 V could be clearly observed in the initial cycle, which is ascribed to the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the electrode surface.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0032_R_d50516580e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0035_R_d50516580e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">35</a>-<a href="#smll201703472-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0037_R_d50516580e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></span> The peak located at 0.32 V during the first discharge process is representative for the sodiation of red P into the P@C-GO/MOF-5 matrix with the formation of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>P phases. Several oxidation peaks located at around 0.58, 0.68, 0.75, and 1.4 V could be clearly observed in the subsequent cycles, attributed to the extraction of sodium ions from Na<sub>3</sub>P phase by a stepwise reaction.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0019_R_d50516580e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Lett.">19</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0035_R_d50516580e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">35</a></span> In the subsequent several cycles, the CV profiles are well overlapped, indicating the enhanced cycling stability after encapsulating red P into the C-GO/MOF-5 matrix. The galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0003">3</a>b, exhibiting typically redox peaks of the red P anode for sodium ion batteries.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0035_R_d50516580e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">35</a></span> The initial coulombic efficiency calculated from the first charge and discharge process is about 62%. The irreversible capacities in the initial cycle could be ascribed to the formation of SEI and the decomposition of electrolyte, which is in agreement with the CV results. The charge–discharge profiles could be fully matched with the CV peaks in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0003">3</a>a.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703472-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc51232e-8eb3-49f5-8867-f611448319ed/smll201703472-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc51232e-8eb3-49f5-8867-f611448319ed/smll201703472-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8d5f67af-096e-4d6e-a700-3a6e77cfa001/smll201703472-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc51232e-8eb3-49f5-8867-f611448319ed/smll201703472-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703472-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703472" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cyclic voltammetry of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode at the potential window between 0.01 and 2 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> with a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) charge and discharge profiles of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode for the first three cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The cycling performance of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode was measured under the galvanostatic charge–discharge at various current densities of 0.1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The specific capacities and various current densities are based on the total mass of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0004">4</a>a shows the cycling performance of the C-GO/MOF-5 and P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrodes at a low current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Compared with the C-GO/MOF-5 electrode, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode exhibits much higher reversible capacity, which is ascribed to the contribution of red P active material in the carbon matrix. A high specific charge capacity of about 1067 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the 2nd cycle could be achieved and could be maintained as high as 939 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 65 cycles. In case of the C-GO/MOF-5 electrode, it only displays a reversible capacity of ≈200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating little capacity contribution from carbon in the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode. For further comparison, pure red P sample was directly used as the anode material for sodium ion battery. As shown in Figure S4a (Supporting Information), when cycled at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the red P anode displays higher discharge/charge capacities of 2284 and 1143 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the initial cycle, but the coulombic efficiency is really unsatisfied, nearly 50%. Moreover, obvious capacity decay in the following several cycles could be clearly observed. The lower charge specific capacity of only 325 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the 5th cycle could be maintained. The severe capacity decay for the pure red P anode could be ascribed to the large volume change and low intrinsic electronic conductivity of pure red P material without the combination of high conductive porous carbon matrix. When the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode is cycled at the current density of 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0004">4</a>b, it exhibits a high reversible capacity of about 830 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with high capacity retention of about 93% after 100 cycles, indicating superior cycling stability. In order to further investigate the cycling performance at higher current density, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode was also measured at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> as shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0004">4</a>c. It is worth noting that the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode maintains a high charge capacity of about 541 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even after 200 cycles.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703472-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/848a097f-7bb7-40ec-b15b-e26a37e2f445/smll201703472-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/848a097f-7bb7-40ec-b15b-e26a37e2f445/smll201703472-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/91a8669f-5af8-422e-a5aa-17a0f6717f48/smll201703472-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/848a097f-7bb7-40ec-b15b-e26a37e2f445/smll201703472-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703472-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703472" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performance of the C-GO/MOF-5 and P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrodes at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; b,c) cycling performance of P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode at the current densities of 2 and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively; d) rate capability of C-GO/MOF-5 and P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrodes at various current densities.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Another attractive feature for the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode is the excellent rate performance. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0004">4</a>d, the reversible charge capacity of 1095, 970, 839, 697, and 555 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be obtained at the current densities of 0.1, 0.5, 2, 5, and 8 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a higher current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode still exhibits high charge capacity of 502 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density returns to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> again, the charge capacity could recover to 940 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating superior cycling performance. However, the pure C-GO/MOF-5 electrode in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0004">4</a>d delivers much lower reversible capacity, indicating the little capacity contribution of the carbon matrix in the composition electrode. For the pure red P anode material in Figure S4b (Supporting Information), it demonstrates rather poor rate performance with severe capacity decay at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and almost no capacity contribution at higher current densities. Compared with our previous work of Li et al.,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0016_R_d50516580e752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a></span> the introduction of the graphene sheets could largely improve the electronic conductivity, facilitating the electron and sodium ion diffusion, which could account for the decent rate performance in this work. In addition, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0005">5</a>, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode demonstrates outstanding rate capability compared with other kinds of red P anode materials based on different matrixes reported recently, such as the phosphorus/graphene hybrid,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0019_R_d50516580e760" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Lett.">19</a></span> carbon/red phosphorus/graphene aerogel 3D architecture,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0017_R_d50516580e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></span> P/C composite,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0038_R_d50516580e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Mater. Chem. A">38</a></span> red phosphorus/porous N-doped carbon nanofibers,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0014_R_d50516580e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></span> red phosphorus@Ni–P core@shell nanostructure,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0039_R_d50516580e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Environ. Sci.">39</a></span> phosphorus@porous multichannel flexible freestanding carbon nanofibers.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0040_R_d50516580e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Storage Mater.">40</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703472-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4015fbed-6790-4a18-9add-0a54faf26511/smll201703472-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4015fbed-6790-4a18-9add-0a54faf26511/smll201703472-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4e7525e5-ae36-49a4-8f98-a70fcd2417fa/smll201703472-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4015fbed-6790-4a18-9add-0a54faf26511/smll201703472-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703472-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703472" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Rate performance of P-based anode materials for sodium ion batteries reported recently. The specific capacities are based on the mass of the red phosphorus.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In order to further investigate the structural stability of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode during cycling, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and SEM images after cycling are performed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0006">6</a>; Table S1 and Figure S5 (Supporting Information), respectively. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703472-fig-0006">6</a> and Table S1 (Supporting Information), the EIS tests were carried out for the fresh cell and after various cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The charge transfer resistance of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode after 20, 30, 40 cycles are 70.91, 97.69, and 98.83 Ω, respectively. Obviously, the charge transfer resistance stays almost unchanged after 30 cycles, indicating the extremely stable SEI film and well-maintained electrical contact,<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0041_R_d50516580e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Commun.">41</a></span> which is ascribed to the superior structural stability of the interconnected porous carbon matrix, further providing a guarantee for the electrochemical stability of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0018_R_d50516580e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a></span> The corresponding SEM images for the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode after 50 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure S5 (Supporting Information). The cube shaped porous structure of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 matrix could be well maintained after cycling, indicating superior structural stability of the carbon matrix, which accounts for the superior electrochemical stability of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703472-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b6e56a2d-6791-48fe-bbbb-d419e85a766b/smll201703472-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b6e56a2d-6791-48fe-bbbb-d419e85a766b/smll201703472-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/70825d5a-52ca-4e45-87a2-14c5c7d9a240/smll201703472-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b6e56a2d-6791-48fe-bbbb-d419e85a766b/smll201703472-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703472-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703472" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Nyquist and fitted plots of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> before and after 20, 30, and 40 cycles. The open-circuit-voltage of electrode was controlled similarly (≈ 2 V).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The superior electrochemical performances of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode for sodium ion batteries are mainly ascribed to the well encapsulation of red P into the cube shaped interconnected porous carbon matrix. First, the combination of the graphene sheets and the porous carbon layer by layer could largely improve the electronic conductivity and further facilitate the sodium ion diffusion into the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode, which enhances the utilization of the red P active material. Second, the interconnected porous structure, high surface area and large pore volume of the C-GO/MOF-5 carbon matrix could effectively accommodate the large volume expansion of red P during the charge and discharge process, facilitate the infiltration of the electrolyte, thus shorten the ion diffusion length and further increase the kinetics of sodium ion diffusion.<span><a href="#smll201703472-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0018_R_d50516580e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a>, <a href="#smll201703472-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703472-bib-0042_R_d50516580e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Electrochim. Acta">42</a></span> What's more, the graphene sheets throughout the cube shaped network structure are beneficial for the structure integrity and stability, which provides a guarantee for the electrochemical stability of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode. Finally, the conductive mesoporous C-GO/MOF-5 framework precisely constrains red P growth within its pores and generates essential electrical contact to the insulating red P, offering the excellent electrochemical performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201703472-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703472-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully encapsulated red P in cube shaped sandwich-like mesoporous carbon matrix (C-GO/MOF-5) through a facile vaporization–condensation method. Excellent sodium storage performances of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode can be achieved using the core (red P)/shell (porous carbon) structure owing to its unique structural characteristics. The cube shaped porous carbon matrix with abundant micro-/mesopores, large pore volume could effectively facilitate the infiltration of the electrolyte, and accommodate large volume expansion of red P during electrochemical process. The excellent conductivity of the C-GO/MOF-5 could ensure the essential electrical contact to the insulating red P, leading to excellent sodium storage performances. As a result, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 electrode shows superior cycling stability with high capacity retention of about 93% at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles and excellent rate performance (502 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>). The rational design of the unique red P/carbon structure could be served as a model for other anode material with poor intrinsic electronic conductivity and large volume expansion.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201703472-sec-0040">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703472-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Preparation of the C-GO/MOF-5</i>: First, graphene oxide (defined as GO) was prepared through the modified Hummers' method with graphite as the precursor. After freeze-dried for 24 h, the GO powder could be obtained and subsequently dispersed in suitable amount <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethyl formamide (DMF) under ultrasonic. Second, Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> · 6H<sub>2</sub>O and 1,4-benzenedicarboxylate (H<sub>2</sub>BDC) were dissolved in DMF, respectively, and mixed with the GO/DMF suspension under stirring. The mixed solution was refluxed at 120 °C for 24 h. The obtained dark product was separated through centrifugation, washed with DMF for several times and then immersed in chloroform for 24 h. After centrifugation from chloroform, the dark sample was dried in an oven for 24 h. Finally, the C-GO/MOF-5 porous carbon could be obtained after carbonization at 800 °C for 5 h under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere and further immersed in 2 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> HCl solution for etching. After the full etching, the dark powder was washed with DI water for several times until a neutral and then dried in an oven for 24 h.</p>
         
         <p><i>Preparation of the P@C-GO/MOF-5 Composite</i>: The P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite was obtained by thermal treatment of the red P and C-GO/MOF-5 through the vaporization–condensation method. The mixture was sealed in a quartz tube under vacuum and further heat treated at 898 °C for 8 h to get the vapor for the diffusion of P into the pores. Then the temperature was maintained at 260 °C for 24 h with a rate of 1 °C min<sup>−1</sup> to realize the fully transformation of white P to red P. Finally, the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite could be successfully obtained after washed with carbon disulfide (CS<sub>2</sub>) for several times to fully remove the residual white P generated during the cooling process. The P@C-GO/MOF-5 powder could be obtained under vacuum drying.</p>
         
         <p><i>Characterization</i>: The morphology and the microstructure of the obtained nanosized material were characterized using the Field-emission SEM (FESEM) with a JSM-6700 FESEM (JEOL, Tokyo, Japan) operated at 5 keV and a JEOL 4000EX TEM (JEOL, Tokyo, Japan). The specific surface area and pore size distribution of the composite were tested with an ASAP 2020 Accelerated Surface Area and Porosimetry Instrument. The crystalline structure was further measured using the XRD (Philips X'pert PRO SUPER) with a Cu Kα radiation and Raman spectroscopy.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Characterization</i>: In order to explore the electrochemical performance of P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite, the CR2032 coin cells were assembled with the P@C-GO/MOF-5 composite as the working electrode and Na metal (purity ≥ 99.5%, SCRC) as counter and reference electrode. The assembly of the half-cells was carried in a glove box (MBRAUN LABMASTER 130) filled with high purity argon atmosphere (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 1 ppm). The working electrode was made of three parts containing the active material, the conductive agent (acetylene black) and polyvinylidene fluoride with the ratio of 8:1:1. Then the mixture was dispersed in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone and the obtained slurry was coated on the copper foil and dried in the vacuum oven at 60 °C for 24 h. The loading of the active material was controlled at about 1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The electrolyte was composed of 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub>, ethylene carbonate (EC), and dimethyl carbonate (1:1, w/w), with 1% volume of fluoroethylene carbonate as the additives. As for the separator, the glass fiber from Whatman was selected due to the large sodium ion radius. The battery test system (Neware BTS-610) was employed to characterized the galvanostatic charge–discharge properties. The CHI 660D electrochemical workstation (Chenhua Instrument Company, Shanghai, China) was used to measure the Cyclic voltammetry and EIS properties in the frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703472-sec-0050-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
